## Letâ€™s talk about sex!

### When you donâ€™t want sex
### å½“ä½ ä¸æƒ³è¦æ€§æ—¶

Letâ€™s make something extremely clear as a baseline: You should never have to have sex when you donâ€™t want to. Not to save a relationship (or have access to intimacy at all), not to show you care, and not to get any of your other needs metâ€”financial (note weâ€™re not talking about sex work here), emotional or social. Not desiring someone physically isnâ€™t a sign that you donâ€™t love them. Or that you want to hurt them. Or that thereâ€™s something wrong with you. Itâ€™s not even a sign that youâ€™re not a compatible partner with them. It just means that, for whatever reason, your body isnâ€™t responding to them. And if you donâ€™t want it, definitely donâ€™t do it.

Mononormative and patriarchal culture instills a number of toxic beliefs about sex and relationships, including:
* You owe sex to someone youâ€™re in a relationship with.
* Sexual desire is something that can be offered or withheld at will.
* A lack of sexual desire is, at best, a sign of something wrong in the relationship. At worst, itâ€™s a sign of malice.

Another is *allonormativity*: the belief that everyone desires sex and experiences sexual attraction, or should. Other â€œnormingâ€ pressures can come into play too, such as heteronormativity (the belief that everyone is or should be heterosexual, or that penis-in-vagina (PIV) is the only â€œrealâ€ kind of sex) and various flavours of gender normativity that pressure everyone to conform to toxic standards of binary gender (â€œa real man always wants sex,â€ â€œa wife always gives it to her husband if she wants to keep him around,â€ and so on).

There are two main situations in which people donâ€™t want sex. One is when a person is on the asexuality spectrum. The other is when a person is allosexual, but their libido has waned in a specific set of circumstances. Weâ€™re going to look at each of these situations in turn.

## ASEXUALITY
## æ— æ€§æ‹ (ASEXUALITY)

Some people who self-identify as asexual or aromantic find nonmonogamy attractive because it allows them to form intimate, loving bonds without the fear that theyâ€™ll experience sexual pressure or deprive their partners of the opportunity for a pleasurable and fulfilling sex life. Emotional closeness, support, love, touch and cuddling can all exist independent of sex. Intimate and even romantic relationships without sex are not â€œmerelyâ€ friendships. They can and do include passionate emotional intimacy, living together, shared goals and dreams, and lifelong plans.

When an asexual person partners with one or more fellow aces, they may have an easier time negotiating the issue of sex. Some asexuals are totally sex-averse, meaning they donâ€™t want sex of any kind ever at all, while some are willing to have (and even enjoy!) certain kinds of sex under certain circumstances. ## DESIRE DISCREPANCY
## æ¬²æœ›å·®å¼‚ (DESIRE DISCREPANCY)

Mismatched libidos are pretty common in relationships. Desire discrepancy can come up for a few reasons. Asexuality is one, of course. But it happens even among allosexuals of all genders. Quite commonly, in fact!

A lot of people (across all genders and sexual orientations) have a flawed understanding of how desire works. Far from being a problem that can be solved by taking a pill, low levels of desire are often related to living in stressful contexts where a personâ€™s arousal â€œbrakesâ€ are constantly primed and their arousal â€œacceleratorsâ€ hard to reach. This can be true at any relationship stage, but it may be particularly noticeable in long-term relationships. In any multi-year partnership, youâ€™re going to end up dealing with the sometimes draining and unpleasant realities of everyday life, which are often not so sexy (brakes!), without the counterbalance of all the fresh and exciting hormones that show up in the early stages of sexual relationships (accelerators!). Also, Western society has normalized the idea of spontaneous desire as the only real kind, whereas for a large percentage of people, desire operates in a more responsive wayâ€”meaning, context matters a lot. Moreover, peopleâ€™s emotions, desires, fantasies and bodies change over time, especially as they age. Sometimes, a thing you used to love in bed just doesnâ€™t do it for you anymore. Sometimes, you discover a new turn-on where you least expect it. Figuring out what you want and like, sexually, is not just a project for young peopleâ€”itâ€™s lifelong.

All this to say that discrepancies in desire are common, in both long-term and newer relationships. And many people, maybe even most people (yes, even the super kinky and adventurous ones!), need to unlearn some of the notions theyâ€™ve absorbed and learn new ones. A fresh perspective and new conceptual tools about sex can work wonders in a relationship if everyone involved is willing to put in the time and effort. A great place to start is Emily Nagoskiâ€™s fantastic book, *Come As You Are*, which discusses brakes and accelerators and other useful concepts, and her recent follow-up, *Come Together*, which focuses on sex in long-term relationships.[^130] Nagoskiâ€™s explanations of how desire works are based on sexuality research that was largely ignored until her first book brought it to light when it was published in 2015. Itâ€™s really worth your time to check out her workâ€”it is truly revolutionary.

Desire discrepancy is something you can work on with a partner. You may be able to find workarounds and new ideas to help you deal with â€œoutsideâ€ situations such as illness or stress. You may be able to deal with the accelerators and brakes, come up with creative solutions for sexual connection when youâ€™re dealing with the limitations of your bodies at this time, even redefine and expand what â€œsexâ€ means to you altogether. However, desire discrepancy can also come up because of eroded or broken trust, communication problems or other relationship issues. In these cases, lack of desire is a symptom of something deeper that might need other kinds of work, for example in a therapistâ€™s office rather than in bed. Each situation is different, and we believe itâ€™s worth digging in to find out what yours in particular is all about before you try to â€œspice up your sex lifeâ€ with new toys or adventures. If it turns out that all you need is a vacation and a vibrator, great! But you might also, or instead, need to prioritize a lot of talking and tenderness, process unresolved conflict or heal festering emotional wounds, or develop and practise new communication skills.

It is also possible, sometimes, for someone to find they are not attracted to their partner at allâ€”either because they never really were, or because of some physical, chemical or hormonal change that caused a once-strong attraction to go away. Long-term conflict or relationship toxicity can also permanently extinguish attraction. In that case, still another kind of conversation is in order, concerning whether the relationship should become nonsexual or transition out of partnership completely.

## Is nonmonogamy a solution for desire discrepancy?
## éå•å¶åˆ¶æ˜¯æ¬²æœ›å·®å¼‚çš„è§£å†³æ–¹æ¡ˆå—ï¼Ÿ

In monogamy, a romantic partner and a sexual partner are, almost by definition, the same person. Emotional intimacy and physical intimacy are so tightly entwined that some self-help books speak of â€œemotional infidelityâ€ and encourage married couples not to permit each other to become too close to their friends. Advice columnists and television personalities will speak gravely of the dangers that â€œemotional affairsâ€ pose to a monogamous marriage and ask, â€œIs emotional infidelity worse than sexual infidelity?â€ Monogamy can leave surprisingly little room for close friendships, much less for nonsexual romances or other kinds of intimacy.

åœ¨å•å¶åˆ¶ä¸­ï¼Œæµªæ¼«ä¼´ä¾£å’Œæ€§ä¼´ä¾£å‡ ä¹æ ¹æ®å®šä¹‰æ˜¯åŒä¸€ä¸ªäººã€‚æƒ…æ„Ÿäº²å¯†å’Œèº«ä½“äº²å¯†å¦‚æ­¤ç´§å¯†åœ°äº¤ç»‡åœ¨ä¸€èµ·ï¼Œä»¥è‡³äºä¸€äº›è‡ªåŠ©ä¹¦ç±è°ˆåˆ°â€œæƒ…æ„Ÿä¸å¿ â€ï¼Œå¹¶é¼“åŠ±å·²å©šå¤«å¦‡ä¸å…è®¸å½¼æ­¤ä¸æœ‹å‹è¿‡äºäº²å¯†ã€‚å»ºè®®ä¸“æ ä½œå®¶å’Œç”µè§†åäººä¼šä¸¥è‚ƒåœ°è°ˆè®ºâ€œæƒ…æ„Ÿå‡ºè½¨â€å¯¹å•å¶åˆ¶å©šå§»æ„æˆçš„å±é™©ï¼Œå¹¶é—®ï¼Œâ€œæƒ…æ„Ÿä¸å¿ æ¯”æ€§ä¸å¿ æ›´ç³Ÿç³•å—ï¼Ÿâ€å•å¶åˆ¶ç»™äº²å¯†å‹è°Šç•™ä¸‹çš„ç©ºé—´å‡ºå¥‡åœ°å°ï¼Œæ›´ä¸ç”¨è¯´éæ€§æµªæ¼«æˆ–å…¶ä»–ç§ç±»çš„äº²å¯†å…³ç³»äº†ã€‚

This creates problems for asexuals and for couples whose members are no longer sexually attracted to one another or have mismatched libidos. If your romantic partner is also expected to be your only sexual partner, and furthermore you are expected to want sex automatically as part of wanting romance, what happens when sexual compatibility isnâ€™t there? What do you do when one person is unwilling or unable to be sexual with the other? In cases like this, monogamy struggles. It seems on the face of it absurd to tell another person â€œIâ€™m not okay with you having your sexual needs met by anyone but me, and I wonâ€™t meet your sexual needs,â€ but thatâ€™s precisely what happens. The monogamous person with unmet sexual needs faces a set of choices: pressuring, coercion, cheating or celibacy.

Even when a good monogamous relationship is nonsexual through mutual choice, it is often treated dismissively, if not derisively. â€œYou and your spouse havenâ€™t had sex in two years? Oh, Iâ€™m so sorry. That must be awful! Whatâ€™s wrong?â€

å³ä½¿ä¸€æ®µè‰¯å¥½çš„å•å¶åˆ¶å…³ç³»é€šè¿‡å…±åŒé€‰æ‹©æˆä¸ºæ— æ€§å…³ç³»ï¼Œå®ƒä¹Ÿç»å¸¸è¢«è½»è§†ï¼Œç”šè‡³å˜²ç¬‘ã€‚â€œä½ å’Œä½ é…å¶ä¸¤å¹´æ²¡åšçˆ±äº†ï¼Ÿå“¦ï¼Œæˆ‘å¾ˆæŠ±æ­‰ã€‚é‚£ä¸€å®šå¾ˆç³Ÿç³•ï¼æ€ä¹ˆäº†ï¼Ÿâ€

One of the advantages of nonmonogamy is that it does not mean hitching all your sexual wagons to a single star. It allows room for change that would threaten the existence of many monogamous relationships. An emotionally satisfying, deeply committed, loving open relationship between two people who are, or have become, sexually incompatible can still flourish. However, the situation demands a careful approach. Sometimes, nonmonogamy can present solutions, for instance by creating space for partners with higher desire levels to explore without placing pressure on lower-desire partners to join in or keep up. At the same time, itâ€™s important to not default to the â€œif the relationship isnâ€™t working, just add more people!â€ approach, which can do a lot of harm to everyone concerned. As we discussed in the last section, desire discrepancies within a relationship can be a signal not that naturally occurring difference is rearing its head, but that something particular to that relationship needs work. So if you simply throw your hands in the air and say â€œOur desires are just too different, guess we better stop trying!â€ you can end up turning away from a sexual relationship that could have been deeply satisfying if youâ€™d only made the effort.

## Emotions
## æƒ…ç»ª (Emotions)

Sex can bring up powerful emotionsâ€”both negative and positive. There can be enormous vulnerability in baring your body, touching another person and being touched, and allowing yourself to explore the unknown or be seen as you experience pleasure. In its best moments, sex can elicit feelings of satisfaction, closeness, silliness, fun, excitement, release, catharsis, even transcendence. In its more difficult ones, you can feel awkward, anxious, uncomfortable, disconnected, sad, angry, ashamed or fearful. You can also map a lot of meaning onto sex. It can symbolize security, love, desirability, worth, competency and much more, which means a lot is riding on it going well.

## ç¾è€» (SHAME)

According to sex therapists Lauren Fogel Mersy and Jennifer A. Vencill in their book *Desire*, sexual shame is â€œthe worry that some part of us is unacceptable, wrong, or bad in the eyes of others,â€ and the feeling that something about us will make others reject us.[^131] They say there are two major sociocultural roots for this kind of feeling. The first is a lack of adequate sex educationâ€”which is sadly all too common today in most countries. Despite the wealth of research and knowledge available, many school jurisdictions provide inadequate sex education or none at all, mostly for political or religious reasons. As far as the United States is concerned, Mersy and Vencill blame religious-right purity culture for much of this, but the problem extends well past the realm of evangelical influence. Around the world, sex ed curricula (when they exist at all) are often shaped by values that have nothing to do with scientific accuracy or an imperative to set young people up for a lifetime of sexual health and well-being and consensual pleasure. They also frequently exclude queer and trans people from the materials entirely, leaving tons of young people ashamed and in the dark even compared to their peersâ€™ limited learning. As a result, lots of people end up feeling scared of sex, ashamed of their bodies, unaware of how to experience pleasure or communicate clearly about their limits and desires, and set up with inadequate or inaccurate information about safer sex.

If that describes your own experience of sex ed as a teenager, you are not alone! The good news is that there are tons of resources available for you to learn from. The slightly less good news is that itâ€™s entirely up to you to seek out this knowledge and challenge yourself to rethink (or unlearn) the things you learned early on. The second factor that gives rise to shame, according to Mersy and Vencill, is â€œan inability to speak openly and honestly about sexual health topics.â€ Which of course ties in directly to the first factor. If youâ€™re not taught about sex in a shame-free, accurate way, especially if youâ€™re also told itâ€™s bad and wrong, then it may be really intimidating to say anything about itâ€”even when talking with someone youâ€™re having sex with! Getting comfortable with talking about sex is a really great step toward taking good care of your own health and the health of the people you have sex with. It might be terrifying at first, but itâ€™s worth working on. If youâ€™re new to this, or just want to improve your sex conversations, you may want to check out the book *Sex Talks* by Vanessa Marin with Xander Marin (itâ€™s not geared for nonmonogamous people, but itâ€™s still got good tips), or work with a coach or sex therapist, alone or with one or more partners.

## TRAUMA
## åˆ›ä¼¤ (TRAUMA)

Statistics tell us that half of all women and nearly a third of men have experienced some kind of physical sexual violence in their lifetime. In addition, trans, gender-nonconforming and queer people are subjected to atrocious levels of violence, much of it gender-based or sexual in nature. Not every experience of violence leads to long-term physical or emotional trauma symptoms, but given these high numbers, itâ€™s not surprising that a lot of peopleâ€™s experiences of sex and sexuality are shaped by trauma. And thatâ€™s not even counting the common experiences of sexual harassment, stalking, catcalling and general objectification that many people are subjected to, especially but not only women. Or the additional levels of violence and discrimination experienced by people with disabilities, Black people, Indigenous people and people of colour, as well as the many people who live at the intersections of multiple oppressions.

What does all this mean, practically speaking? A lot of things, but two in particular stand out.

## Consent, boundaries and communication
## åŒæ„ã€ç•Œé™ä¸æ²Ÿé€š (Consent, boundaries and communication)

Sex is the erotic experience or exchange that happens within all participantsâ€™ boundaries and subject to all participantsâ€™ consent. You may communicate about it a lot or not very much, and you might enjoy it a lot or not very muchâ€”sex can be a messy affair in which you make mistakes, learn about yourself and others, and figure out how to make the best of things when they go wrong. Bodies are messy and leaky, furniture can break, sex toys can go on the fritz, safer sex supplies can fail, and you might run out of lube or get a phone call from one of the kids at exactly the wrong moment. You might also encounter a boundary or trigger you didnâ€™t know was there, in yourself or someone else, and have to navigate that in the moment with as much care and sensitivity as possible. But if everyone involved communicates clearly and respects each otherâ€™s boundaries and consent, it tends to reduce the potential for damage and boost the potential for pleasure and connection, even when life interferes.

## CONSENT
## åŒæ„ (CONSENT)

A lot of people arenâ€™t familiar with this legal definition of consent. Itâ€™s a lot stricter than many might expect! For that reason alone, we think itâ€™s worth setting it out here.

In Canada, the law is very clear in stating that sexual activity requires an affirmative, voluntary agreement to be considered consensual. That means consent is active, not silent or passive, and not just a failure to say no. The law also makes it clear that consenting to one act doesnâ€™t mean youâ€™re consenting to everything else someone might want to do with you. It also says that if you donâ€™t have the full capacity to give consentâ€”say, if youâ€™re drunk or high or asleep, or your judgment is otherwise impairedâ€”then your consent isnâ€™t considered valid. It also doesnâ€™t count as consent if the other person uses their position of power or authority to coerce you into a yes.[^134]

But legalities arenâ€™t the only important thing. Among other things, they tend to be based on the idea that everyone holds equal negotiating power, except in cases where someone is in a position of official power or authority over someone else (say, a teacher and a student). This is what might be called a â€œliberalâ€ notion of equality under the law. This legal framework, robust as it may be, fails to account for differences along the lines of social privilege: for instance, if one person is physically disabled and another not, or if one person is more sexually experienced or better known in a given community. You can think critically about these kinds of power differences and negotiate with them in mind; you can do better than just respecting the law. Finally, the legal framework does not address the murky areas of misunderstandings, activity that was consented to (even enthusiastically) but that caused harm anyway, and re-evaluation of your experiences as you grow and learn. As A.V. Flox writes:[^135]

*In a legal model, even one that attempts to expand our understanding of consent like affirmative consent does, there is no space for people to reflect on what theyâ€™ve done and honor the evolution of their feelings about it or their partner in a wider context. This is because the legal model of consent is one that primarily focuses on whether static rules are being broken, rather than one that centers on the individuals, who are by nature dynamic.*

Robin Bauer, a scholar who studies dyke and queer BDSM communities, calls for people to establish what he calls *critical consent*. He defines this as â€œan active, ongoing collaboration for the mutual benefit of all involved, helping to establish and maintain each participantâ€™s own sense of integrity.â€ A critical consent approach, as he explains it, comes with a heightened sense of responsibility, the willingness to be accountable for the consequences of your actions, and a sense of increased sexual agency thatâ€™s not based on pretending that social hierarchies and norms donâ€™t exist. Rather, he calls for people to work together on critical consent practices, â€œfor instance through the insight that, for consent to be valid, actors do not need to be equal (which is practically impossible), but do need to be able to access negotiating power in that particular situation.â€[^136]

Practically speaking, that means you need to acknowledge the power differentials between yourself and the partners youâ€™re having sex with, and then negotiate in full awareness of those differences. ## BOUNDARIES AND COMMUNICATION
## ç•Œé™ä¸æ²Ÿé€š (BOUNDARIES AND COMMUNICATION)

Weâ€™ve discussed boundary-setting in several chapters of this book, including chapters 2 and 3. We wonâ€™t repeat all the general concepts here, but of course sex is another place where boundaries come up. Those boundaries may be emotional, such as needing certain accommodations for trauma triggers or checking in before certain kinds of touch, or physical, such as asking that your partners use condoms or gloves for certain acts or respect your physical limitations around pain or disability. And just as anywhere else, your boundary is about what you will permit to happenâ€”not about controlling another personâ€™s behaviour. So for example, you can say â€œI wonâ€™t have PIV sex with you if youâ€™re having unprotected PIV sex with others,â€ because thatâ€™s about your own risk tolerance. But itâ€™s no longer boundary-setting when you say â€œYouâ€™re not allowed to have PIV sex with anyone but meâ€â€”thatâ€™s crossing the line into imposing rules on othersâ€™ behaviour.

To set sex-related boundaries, it helps to know what you like and donâ€™t like, and to be able to tell a sexual partner about those things. But how do you figure out what you like and donâ€™t like? Do people come preprogrammed with that knowledge? In some cases, yes, in the sense that you can feel disgust or desire toward a particular act (or person) without having direct experience to back it up or confirm it. A great example is sexual orientation. People often know, even in childhood, that theyâ€™re drawn to others of a certain gender presentation, and this knowledge is valid and real. That doesnâ€™t mean what you like is always stable and never changes, but it does mean that some major features of your desires may be clear to you long before you confirm them through direct experience.

## Risk assessment
## é£é™©è¯„ä¼° (Risk assessment)

Fact: You are terrible at objectively assessing risk. So are we, and so is everyone youâ€™re likely to meet. Our brains are poor at evaluating real risk vs. perceived risk. We fear riding in airplanes, but get into cars, which are a more dangerous way to travel, without a second thought. Our emotional assessment of risk is strongly skewed toward spectacular but unlikely scenarios (sharks!) and biased away from situations where we feel a sense of control (lighting a match). Our brains are also terrible at understanding probability, which leads us to irrational decisions. For example, if you drive 15 kilometres to buy a lottery ticket, you are far more likely to be killed in a car crash getting there than to win the lottery. Furthermore, research has demonstrated that our perception of risk is collective; it relies more on the particular social group we are part of than on the actual level of risk.

äº‹å®ï¼šä½ åœ¨å®¢è§‚è¯„ä¼°é£é™©æ–¹é¢å¾ˆç³Ÿç³•ã€‚æˆ‘ä»¬ä¹Ÿæ˜¯ï¼Œä½ å¯èƒ½é‡åˆ°çš„æ¯ä¸ªäººä¹Ÿæ˜¯ã€‚æˆ‘ä»¬çš„å¤§è„‘ä¸å–„äºè¯„ä¼°çœŸå®é£é™©ä¸æ„ŸçŸ¥é£é™©ã€‚æˆ‘ä»¬å®³æ€•åé£æœºï¼Œå´ä¸å‡æ€ç´¢åœ°åè¿›æ±½è½¦ï¼Œè€Œæ±½è½¦æ˜¯ä¸€ç§æ›´å±é™©çš„æ—…è¡Œæ–¹å¼ã€‚æˆ‘ä»¬å¯¹é£é™©çš„æƒ…æ„Ÿè¯„ä¼°å¼ºçƒˆåå‘äºå£®è§‚ä½†ä¸å¤ªå¯èƒ½å‘ç”Ÿçš„åœºæ™¯ï¼ˆé²¨é±¼ï¼ï¼‰ï¼Œå¹¶åç¦»æˆ‘ä»¬æ„Ÿåˆ°æœ‰æ§åˆ¶æ„Ÿçš„æƒ…å†µï¼ˆåˆ’ç«æŸ´ï¼‰ã€‚æˆ‘ä»¬çš„å¤§è„‘ä¹Ÿå¾ˆéš¾ç†è§£æ¦‚ç‡ï¼Œè¿™å¯¼è‡´æˆ‘ä»¬åšå‡ºéç†æ€§çš„å†³å®šã€‚ä¾‹å¦‚ï¼Œå¦‚æœä½ å¼€è½¦ 15 å…¬é‡Œå»ä¹°å½©ç¥¨ï¼Œä½ åœ¨å»é‚£é‡Œçš„è·¯ä¸Šæ­»äºè½¦ç¥¸çš„å¯èƒ½æ€§è¿œå¤§äºä¸­å½©ç¥¨çš„å¯èƒ½æ€§ã€‚æ­¤å¤–ï¼Œç ”ç©¶è¡¨æ˜ï¼Œæˆ‘ä»¬å¯¹é£é™©çš„æ„ŸçŸ¥æ˜¯é›†ä½“çš„ï¼›å®ƒæ›´å¤šåœ°ä¾èµ–äºæˆ‘ä»¬æ‰€å±çš„ç‰¹å®šç¤¾ä¼šç¾¤ä½“ï¼Œè€Œä¸æ˜¯å®é™…çš„é£é™©æ°´å¹³ã€‚

This inability to assess risk applies just as strongly to sexual health as to anything else in our lives. We fear AIDS but not hepatitis, even though hepatitis is more common and kills more people worldwide every year. Add to that the stigma associated with sexual health, and itâ€™s no surprise that realistic assessment of sexually transmitted infection (STI) risk is difficult. We tend to treat someone who has had gonorrhea very differently than someone who has had strep throat, even though both are bacterial infections that are sometimes antibiotic-resistant, sometimes dangerous, but generally treatable.

è¿™ç§æ— æ³•è¯„ä¼°é£é™©çš„èƒ½åŠ›åŒæ ·å¼ºçƒˆåœ°é€‚ç”¨äºæ€§å¥åº·ï¼Œå°±åƒé€‚ç”¨äºæˆ‘ä»¬ç”Ÿæ´»ä¸­çš„å…¶ä»–ä»»ä½•äº‹æƒ…ä¸€æ ·ã€‚æˆ‘ä»¬å®³æ€•è‰¾æ»‹ç—…ä½†ä¸å®³æ€•è‚ç‚ï¼Œå°½ç®¡è‚ç‚æ›´å¸¸è§ä¸”æ¯å¹´åœ¨å…¨ä¸–ç•Œå¯¼è‡´æ›´å¤šäººæ­»äº¡ã€‚åŠ ä¸Šä¸æ€§å¥åº·ç›¸å…³çš„æ±¡åï¼Œå¯¹æ€§ä¼ æ’­æ„ŸæŸ“ (STI) é£é™©è¿›è¡Œç°å®è¯„ä¼°å¾ˆå›°éš¾ä¹Ÿå°±ä¸è¶³ä¸ºå¥‡äº†ã€‚æˆ‘ä»¬å¯¹å¾…æ·‹ç—…æ‚£è€…çš„æ–¹å¼å¾€å¾€ä¸å¯¹å¾…é“¾çƒèŒæ€§å’½å–‰ç‚æ‚£è€…æˆªç„¶ä¸åŒï¼Œå°½ç®¡ä¸¤è€…éƒ½æ˜¯ç»†èŒæ„ŸæŸ“ï¼Œæœ‰æ—¶å…·æœ‰æŠ—ç”Ÿç´ è€è¯æ€§ï¼Œæœ‰æ—¶å¾ˆå±é™©ï¼Œä½†é€šå¸¸æ˜¯å¯ä»¥æ²»ç–—çš„ã€‚

Many people react with horror to a disclosure that someone has something common like herpes. Many people say, â€œI would never even consider a partner with herpes!â€ even though, ironically, perhaps half (or more) of the people who say that actually have herpes themselves and just donâ€™t know it. Depending on where you live in the world, anywhere from 50 to 90 percent of the population has HSV-1 (typically oral herpes) or HSV-2 (typically genital herpes), but the vast majority of those people are not aware they have it. Many of these people are asymptomatic or have one outbreak, easily missed, and never have an outbreak again.

On top of our skewed perceptions about the risks of specific practices or of specific STIs, our emotional perception of risk makes us likely to rate risk higher when we have no direct benefit from it than when we do. This means that youâ€™re likely to feel more afraid when a partner has other sexual partners than when you have other sexual partners yourself, even if the risk profile is the same, and even though you have an extra degree of separation from your partnerâ€™s sexual partners.

Like driving a car or climbing a ladder, there is no way to guarantee sex will be absolutely safe. Even if previously celibate people start a totally monogamous relationship, that is not a guarantee. Many nominally sexually transmitted infections, including herpes and human papillomavirus (HPV), are often transmitted nonsexually as well. For example, more people contract herpes 1 (often expressed as oral cold sores) by nonsexual means than by sexual means, usually during childhood. And by far the riskiest infection today, in terms of prevalence and likelihood of long-term damage, is COVID-19, which is transmitted in a wide variety of social (and sexual) situations, and which, regrettably, few people anymore take even the most nominal measures to prevent or negotiate around.

å°±åƒå¼€è½¦æˆ–çˆ¬æ¢¯å­ä¸€æ ·ï¼Œæ²¡æœ‰åŠæ³•ä¿è¯æ€§çˆ±ç»å¯¹å®‰å…¨ã€‚å³ä½¿ä»¥å‰ç¦æ¬²çš„äººå¼€å§‹ä¸€æ®µå®Œå…¨å•å¶åˆ¶çš„å…³ç³»ï¼Œè¿™ä¹Ÿä¸æ˜¯ä¿è¯ã€‚è®¸å¤šåä¹‰ä¸Šçš„æ€§ä¼ æ’­æ„ŸæŸ“ï¼ŒåŒ…æ‹¬ç–±ç–¹å’Œäººä¹³å¤´ç˜¤ç—…æ¯’ (HPV)ï¼Œé€šå¸¸ä¹Ÿé€šè¿‡éæ€§æ–¹å¼ä¼ æ’­ã€‚ä¾‹å¦‚ï¼Œé€šè¿‡éæ€§æ–¹å¼æ„ŸæŸ“ 1 å‹ç–±ç–¹ï¼ˆé€šå¸¸è¡¨ç°ä¸ºå£è…”å”‡ç–±ç–¹ï¼‰çš„äººæ¯”é€šè¿‡æ€§æ–¹å¼æ„ŸæŸ“çš„äººå¤šï¼Œé€šå¸¸æ˜¯åœ¨å„¿ç«¥æ—¶æœŸã€‚å°±æµè¡Œç¨‹åº¦å’Œé€ æˆé•¿æœŸæŸå®³çš„å¯èƒ½æ€§è€Œè¨€ï¼Œç›®å‰é£é™©æœ€å¤§çš„æ„ŸæŸ“æ˜¯ COVID-19ï¼Œå®ƒåœ¨å„ç§å„æ ·çš„ç¤¾äº¤ï¼ˆå’Œæ€§ï¼‰åœºåˆä¼ æ’­ï¼Œä»¤äººé—æ†¾çš„æ˜¯ï¼Œç°åœ¨å¾ˆå°‘æœ‰äººé‡‡å–å“ªæ€•æ˜¯æœ€åä¹‰ä¸Šçš„æªæ–½æ¥é¢„é˜²æˆ–åå•†ã€‚

Given that sex carries some degree of risk, the real question isnâ€™t â€œHow can we be totally safe?â€ but rather â€œWhat level of risk is acceptable?â€ Different people have very different answers.

é‰´äºæ€§è¡Œä¸ºå¸¦æœ‰ä¸€å®šç¨‹åº¦çš„é£é™©ï¼ŒçœŸæ­£çš„é—®é¢˜ä¸æ˜¯â€œæˆ‘ä»¬å¦‚ä½•æ‰èƒ½ç»å¯¹å®‰å…¨ï¼Ÿâ€è€Œæ˜¯â€œä»€ä¹ˆç¨‹åº¦çš„é£é™©æ˜¯å¯ä»¥æ¥å—çš„ï¼Ÿâ€ä¸åŒçš„äººæœ‰éå¸¸ä¸åŒçš„ç­”æ¡ˆã€‚

## Negotiating risk tolerance
## åå•†é£é™©æ‰¿å—èƒ½åŠ› (Negotiating risk tolerance)

When talking about safer-sex boundaries and risk tolerance, remember thereâ€™s no one right answer. Everyoneâ€™s threshold of acceptable risk is different, and people use different metrics for assessing risk. For example, if you have a compromised immune system, you might be very conservative with risk-taking concerning sexually and nonsexually transmitted infections. The same might be true if youâ€™re a single parent or living on a low income and have to reduce the likelihood of getting ill as much as you possibly can, because you donâ€™t have much of a financial cushion or the ability to take time off work. Or maybe youâ€™ve had a previous experience of being seriously ill and have vowed to never repeat it if you have any say in the matter.

åœ¨è°ˆè®ºå®‰å…¨æ€§è¡Œä¸ºç•Œé™å’Œé£é™©æ‰¿å—èƒ½åŠ›æ—¶ï¼Œè¯·è®°ä½æ²¡æœ‰å”¯ä¸€çš„æ­£ç¡®ç­”æ¡ˆã€‚æ¯ä¸ªäººå¯æ¥å—çš„é£é™©é˜ˆå€¼éƒ½ä¸åŒï¼Œäººä»¬ä½¿ç”¨ä¸åŒçš„æŒ‡æ ‡æ¥è¯„ä¼°é£é™©ã€‚ä¾‹å¦‚ï¼Œå¦‚æœä½ å…ç–«ç³»ç»Ÿå—æŸï¼Œä½ å¯èƒ½åœ¨æ€§ä¼ æ’­å’Œéæ€§ä¼ æ’­æ„ŸæŸ“çš„é£é™©æ‰¿æ‹…æ–¹é¢éå¸¸ä¿å®ˆã€‚å¦‚æœä½ æ˜¯å•äº²çˆ¶æ¯æˆ–ä½æ”¶å…¥è€…ï¼Œå¿…é¡»å°½å¯èƒ½é™ä½ç”Ÿç—…çš„å¯èƒ½æ€§ï¼Œå› ä¸ºä½ æ²¡æœ‰å¤ªå¤šçš„ç»æµç¼“å†²æˆ–è¯·å‡çš„èƒ½åŠ›ï¼Œæƒ…å†µå¯èƒ½ä¹Ÿæ˜¯å¦‚æ­¤ã€‚æˆ–è€…ä¹Ÿè®¸ä½ æœ‰è¿‡é‡ç—…çš„ç»å†ï¼Œå¹¶å‘èª“å¦‚æœèƒ½åšä¸»å°±ç»ä¸é‡è¹ˆè¦†è¾™ã€‚

Everyone must decide on the degree of physical risk they are willing to accept in their own sex and relationship lives. This decision is an important part of acting with agency. Each person is responsible for protecting their own health, and that includes making decisions about what risks they will accept. Part of that decision will be emotional, and thatâ€™s okay.

æ¯ä¸ªäººéƒ½å¿…é¡»å†³å®šä»–ä»¬åœ¨è‡ªå·±çš„æ€§å’Œå…³ç³»ç”Ÿæ´»ä¸­æ„¿æ„æ¥å—çš„èº«ä½“é£é™©ç¨‹åº¦ã€‚è¿™ä¸ªå†³å®šæ˜¯è¡Œä½¿ä»£ç†æƒçš„é‡è¦ç»„æˆéƒ¨åˆ†ã€‚æ¯ä¸ªäººéƒ½è¦è´Ÿè´£ä¿æŠ¤è‡ªå·±çš„å¥åº·ï¼Œè¿™åŒ…æ‹¬å†³å®šä»–ä»¬å°†æ¥å—å“ªäº›é£é™©ã€‚è¿™ä¸ªå†³å®šçš„ä¸€éƒ¨åˆ†å°†æ˜¯æƒ…æ„Ÿä¸Šçš„ï¼Œè¿™æ²¡å…³ç³»ã€‚

Just as you have the right to choose your own level of acceptable risk, so do others. Shaming other people for their choices is not good behaviour. This includes shaming people for making choices that are not only more conservative than yours, but also less conservative. Itâ€™s fine to choose not to be involved with someone whose risk threshold is higher than yours, but that doesnâ€™t make such a person untrustworthy, reckless or foolish. When you contribute to the stigma surrounding sexual choices and STIs, itâ€™s actively harmful. Shame is one of the key factors that prevents people from seeking out testing and treatment, so the less shaming you do, the more likely people around you will take better care of their health.

å°±åƒä½ æœ‰æƒé€‰æ‹©è‡ªå·±å¯æ¥å—çš„é£é™©æ°´å¹³ä¸€æ ·ï¼Œå…¶ä»–äººä¹Ÿæœ‰æƒã€‚å› ä¸ºåˆ«äººçš„é€‰æ‹©è€Œç¾è¾±ä»–ä»¬ä¸æ˜¯å¥½çš„è¡Œä¸ºã€‚è¿™åŒ…æ‹¬ç¾è¾±é‚£äº›åšå‡ºä¸ä»…æ¯”ä½ ä¿å®ˆï¼Œè€Œä¸”æ¯”ä½ æ›´ä¸ä¿å®ˆçš„é€‰æ‹©çš„äººã€‚é€‰æ‹©ä¸ä¸é£é™©é˜ˆå€¼æ¯”ä½ é«˜çš„äººäº¤å¾€æ˜¯å¯ä»¥çš„ï¼Œä½†è¿™å¹¶ä¸æ„å‘³ç€é‚£æ ·çš„äººä¸å€¼å¾—ä¿¡ä»»ã€é²è½æˆ–æ„šè ¢ã€‚å½“ä½ åŠ©é•¿å›´ç»•æ€§é€‰æ‹©å’Œ STI çš„æ±¡åæ—¶ï¼Œè¿™æ˜¯åœ¨ç§¯æåœ°é€ æˆä¼¤å®³ã€‚ç¾è€»æ„Ÿæ˜¯é˜»ç¢äººä»¬å¯»æ±‚æ£€æµ‹å’Œæ²»ç–—çš„å…³é”®å› ç´ ä¹‹ä¸€ï¼Œæ‰€ä»¥ä½ è¶Šå°‘ç¾è¾±åˆ«äººï¼Œä½ å‘¨å›´çš„äººå°±è¶Šæœ‰å¯èƒ½æ›´å¥½åœ°ç…§é¡¾è‡ªå·±çš„å¥åº·ã€‚

## Fluid bonding: Itâ€™s not a thing
## ä½“æ¶²ç»“åˆï¼šè¿™ä¸æ˜¯ä¸ªä¸œè¥¿ (Fluid bonding: Itâ€™s not a thing)

One idea youâ€™re likely to come across in nonmonogamous circles is that of the *fluid bond*. Essentially, the idea is that once youâ€™ve all shared STI test results and discussed risks and so on, you might agree to have unbarriered sex with one or more partners (meaning you donâ€™t use condoms, dental dams or gloves), creating a â€œbondâ€ that allows you to share bodily fluids without worry among yourselves. To maintain that worry-free zone, you all agree to use protection with others with whom you are not fluid bonded.

åœ¨éå•å¶åˆ¶åœˆå­é‡Œï¼Œä½ å¾ˆå¯èƒ½ä¼šé‡åˆ°çš„ä¸€ä¸ªæ¦‚å¿µæ˜¯**ä½“æ¶²ç»“åˆ** (fluid bond)ã€‚æœ¬è´¨ä¸Šï¼Œè¿™ä¸ªæƒ³æ³•æ˜¯ï¼Œä¸€æ—¦ä½ ä»¬éƒ½åˆ†äº«äº† STI æ£€æµ‹ç»“æœå¹¶è®¨è®ºäº†é£é™©ç­‰ç­‰ï¼Œä½ ä»¬å¯èƒ½ä¼šåŒæ„ä¸ä¸€ä¸ªæˆ–å¤šä¸ªä¼´ä¾£è¿›è¡Œæ— ä¿æŠ¤æ€§è¡Œä¸ºï¼ˆæ„å‘³ç€ä½ ä»¬ä¸ä½¿ç”¨é¿å­•å¥—ã€å£äº¤è†œæˆ–æ‰‹å¥—ï¼‰ï¼Œä»è€Œåˆ›é€ ä¸€ç§â€œç»“åˆâ€ï¼Œå…è®¸ä½ ä»¬åœ¨å½¼æ­¤ä¹‹é—´æ— å¿§æ— è™‘åœ°åˆ†äº«ä½“æ¶²ã€‚ä¸ºäº†ç»´æŒè¿™ä¸ªæ— å¿§åŒºï¼Œä½ ä»¬éƒ½åŒæ„ä¸æœªå»ºç«‹ä½“æ¶²ç»“åˆçš„å…¶ä»–äººä½¿ç”¨ä¿æŠ¤æªæ–½ã€‚

Unfortunately, while itâ€™s a popular concept, fluid bonding is not a scientific concept. Why? A bunch of reasons! To begin with, itâ€™s based on the kind of advice that doctors often give to patients based on assumed monogamy: the idea that once youâ€™ve settled down in a stable partnership, youâ€™re only dealing with two peopleâ€™s bodies, fluids and behaviours. But even monogamous people transmit STIs to each other. Even if two people whoâ€™ve never had sex before decide to become a monogamous pair and stay that way for life, they may each bring in viruses that are transmissible by sexual means. And how many of us show up in a monogamous relationship with zero previous sexual experience? Itâ€™s vanishingly rare today.

ä¸å¹¸çš„æ˜¯ï¼Œè™½ç„¶è¿™æ˜¯ä¸€ä¸ªæµè¡Œçš„æ¦‚å¿µï¼Œä½†ä½“æ¶²ç»“åˆå¹¶ä¸æ˜¯ä¸€ä¸ªç§‘å­¦æ¦‚å¿µã€‚ä¸ºä»€ä¹ˆï¼ŸåŸå› å¾ˆå¤šï¼é¦–å…ˆï¼Œå®ƒæ˜¯åŸºäºåŒ»ç”Ÿé€šå¸¸åŸºäºå‡è®¾çš„å•å¶åˆ¶ç»™ç—…äººçš„é‚£ç§å»ºè®®ï¼šå³ä¸€æ—¦ä½ åœ¨ç¨³å®šçš„ä¼´ä¾£å…³ç³»ä¸­å®‰å®šä¸‹æ¥ï¼Œä½ å°±åªåœ¨å¤„ç†ä¸¤ä¸ªäººçš„èº«ä½“ã€ä½“æ¶²å’Œè¡Œä¸ºã€‚ä½†å³ä½¿æ˜¯å•å¶åˆ¶çš„äººä¹Ÿä¼šäº’ç›¸ä¼ æ’­ STIã€‚å³ä½¿ä¸¤ä¸ªä»æœªæœ‰è¿‡æ€§è¡Œä¸ºçš„äººå†³å®šç»“æˆå•å¶åˆ¶ä¼´ä¾£å¹¶ç»ˆèº«ä¿æŒè¿™ç§çŠ¶æ€ï¼Œä»–ä»¬æ¯ä¸ªäººéƒ½å¯èƒ½å¸¦å…¥å¯é€šè¿‡æ€§é€”å¾„ä¼ æ’­çš„ç—…æ¯’ã€‚è€Œä¸”æˆ‘ä»¬ä¸­æœ‰å¤šå°‘äººåœ¨å•å¶åˆ¶å…³ç³»ä¸­å‡ºç°æ—¶æ˜¯é›¶æ€§ç»éªŒçš„ï¼Ÿè¿™åœ¨ä»Šå¤©æå…¶ç½•è§ã€‚

Next, many STIs are transmitted in ways that donâ€™t involve bodily fluids. For example, genital warts spread via skin-to-skin contact, so using barriers offers only partial protection unless those barriers cover the entire genital areaâ€”which condoms and dental dams donâ€™t. In another example, mpox also spreads via skin-to-skin contact, often around the genitals, but also any other skin. When mpox outbreaks began to occur in North America in 2022, they hit gay menâ€™s communities in particularâ€”but some guys contracted it without having sex, simply from spending time at bars where people brushed against each other in close quarters. Outbreaks occurred in elementary school classrooms for that same reason.

å…¶æ¬¡ï¼Œè®¸å¤š STI çš„ä¼ æ’­æ–¹å¼ä¸æ¶‰åŠä½“æ¶²ã€‚ä¾‹å¦‚ï¼Œç”Ÿæ®–å™¨ç–£é€šè¿‡çš®è‚¤æ¥è§¦ä¼ æ’­ï¼Œæ‰€ä»¥é™¤éå±éšœè¦†ç›–æ•´ä¸ªç”Ÿæ®–å™¨åŒºåŸŸâ€”â€”é¿å­•å¥—å’Œå£äº¤è†œåšä¸åˆ°â€”â€”å¦åˆ™ä½¿ç”¨å±éšœåªèƒ½æä¾›éƒ¨åˆ†ä¿æŠ¤ã€‚å¦ä¸€ä¸ªä¾‹å­æ˜¯ï¼ŒçŒ´ç—˜ä¹Ÿé€šè¿‡çš®è‚¤æ¥è§¦ä¼ æ’­ï¼Œé€šå¸¸åœ¨ç”Ÿæ®–å™¨å‘¨å›´ï¼Œä½†ä¹ŸåŒ…æ‹¬ä»»ä½•å…¶ä»–çš®è‚¤ã€‚å½“ 2022 å¹´åŒ—ç¾å¼€å§‹çˆ†å‘çŒ´ç—˜ç–«æƒ…æ—¶ï¼Œå®ƒç‰¹åˆ«è¢­å‡»äº†ç”·åŒæ€§æ‹ç¤¾åŒºâ€”â€”ä½†æœ‰äº›äººåœ¨æ²¡æœ‰å‘ç”Ÿæ€§å…³ç³»çš„æƒ…å†µä¸‹æ„ŸæŸ“äº†å®ƒï¼Œä»…ä»…æ˜¯å› ä¸ºåœ¨é…’å§é‡Œäººä»¬åœ¨ç‹­çª„çš„ç©ºé—´é‡Œäº’ç›¸æ‘©æ“¦ã€‚å‡ºäºåŒæ ·çš„åŸå› ï¼Œå°å­¦æ•™å®¤é‡Œä¹Ÿçˆ†å‘äº†ç–«æƒ…ã€‚

Third, getting a slate of negative STI tests doesnâ€™t guarantee that you have zero STIs. Your doctor will likely recommend what to test for based on what â€œrisk groupsâ€ you belong toâ€”and even if you ask, some insurance wonâ€™t cover certain tests outside those risk groups. For example, in many places itâ€™s not recommended to test for HPV in people under 30 or who donâ€™t have a cervix, in part because HPV is common and often goes away on its own. There is also a range of less common STIs, as well as ones for which tests are not routinely conducted or for which testing is not always of great practical use. For example, a herpes swab might (but wonâ€™t always) tell you if a sore you have is caused by a herpes virus, but that doesnâ€™t help if you donâ€™t have any sores; many people are asymptomatic. You can get a herpes blood test, which might (but wonâ€™t always) tell you if youâ€™ve been exposed to the virus, but the blood test canâ€™t tell you if a specific sore you have is caused by herpes, and you can transmit the virus even if you never have a sore anywhere.[^141]

ç¬¬ä¸‰ï¼Œè·å¾—ä¸€ç³»åˆ—é˜´æ€§çš„ STI æ£€æµ‹ç»“æœå¹¶ä¸èƒ½ä¿è¯ä½ æ²¡æœ‰ STIã€‚ä½ çš„åŒ»ç”Ÿå¯èƒ½ä¼šæ ¹æ®ä½ æ‰€å±çš„â€œé£é™©ç¾¤ä½“â€å»ºè®®æ£€æµ‹ä»€ä¹ˆâ€”â€”å³ä½¿ä½ è¦æ±‚ï¼Œæœ‰äº›ä¿é™©ä¹Ÿä¸ä¼šæ‰¿ä¿é‚£äº›é£é™©ç¾¤ä½“ä¹‹å¤–çš„æŸäº›æ£€æµ‹ã€‚ä¾‹å¦‚ï¼Œåœ¨è®¸å¤šåœ°æ–¹ï¼Œä¸å»ºè®®å¯¹ 30 å²ä»¥ä¸‹æˆ–æ²¡æœ‰å®«é¢ˆçš„äººè¿›è¡Œ HPV æ£€æµ‹ï¼Œéƒ¨åˆ†åŸå› æ˜¯ HPV å¾ˆå¸¸è§ä¸”é€šå¸¸ä¼šè‡ªè¡Œæ¶ˆå¤±ã€‚è¿˜æœ‰ä¸€ç³»åˆ—ä¸å¤ªå¸¸è§çš„ STIï¼Œä»¥åŠä¸å¸¸è§„è¿›è¡Œæ£€æµ‹çš„ STIï¼Œæˆ–è€…æ£€æµ‹å¹¶ä¸æ€»æ˜¯æœ‰å¾ˆå¤§å®é™…ç”¨é€”çš„ STIã€‚ä¾‹å¦‚ï¼Œç–±ç–¹æ‹­å­å¯èƒ½ï¼ˆä½†å¹¶ä¸æ€»æ˜¯ï¼‰å‘Šè¯‰ä½ ä½ çš„ç–®æ˜¯å¦ç”±ç–±ç–¹ç—…æ¯’å¼•èµ·ï¼Œä½†è¿™å¦‚æœä½ æ²¡æœ‰ä»»ä½•ç–®å°±æ²¡æœ‰å¸®åŠ©ï¼›è®¸å¤šäººæ˜¯æ— ç—‡çŠ¶çš„ã€‚ä½ å¯ä»¥è¿›è¡Œç–±ç–¹è¡€æ¶²æ£€æµ‹ï¼Œè¿™å¯èƒ½ï¼ˆä½†å¹¶ä¸æ€»æ˜¯ï¼‰å‘Šè¯‰ä½ æ˜¯å¦æ¥è§¦è¿‡è¯¥ç—…æ¯’ï¼Œä½†è¡€æ¶²æ£€æµ‹æ— æ³•å‘Šè¯‰ä½ ç‰¹å®šçš„ç–®æ˜¯å¦ç”±ç–±ç–¹å¼•èµ·ï¼Œè€Œä¸”å³ä½¿ä½ ä»æœªåœ¨ä»»ä½•åœ°æ–¹é•¿è¿‡ç–®ï¼Œä½ ä¹Ÿå¯èƒ½ä¼ æ’­è¯¥ç—…æ¯’ã€‚[^141]

Lastly, fluid bonding doesnâ€™t take into account the three elements involved in STI transmission: the virus in question, the bodily fluid in question, and the specific behaviours you engage in. For example, the insertive and receptive partners in anal sex are at very different degrees of risk for HIV transmission. And itâ€™s easier to transmit hepatitis C through blood than through sexual fluids, meaning youâ€™re more likely to catch it from borrowing someoneâ€™s razor than from engaging in oral sex with themâ€”but razor-sharing isnâ€™t usually part of the fluid bonding discussion.

æœ€åï¼Œä½“æ¶²ç»“åˆæ²¡æœ‰è€ƒè™‘åˆ°æ¶‰åŠ STI ä¼ æ’­çš„ä¸‰ä¸ªè¦ç´ ï¼šç›¸å…³çš„ç—…æ¯’ã€ç›¸å…³çš„ä½“æ¶²ä»¥åŠä½ ä»äº‹çš„å…·ä½“è¡Œä¸ºã€‚ä¾‹å¦‚ï¼Œè‚›äº¤ä¸­çš„æ’å…¥æ–¹å’Œæ¥å—æ–¹åœ¨ HIV ä¼ æ’­é£é™©ç¨‹åº¦ä¸Šæœ‰å¾ˆå¤§ä¸åŒã€‚è€Œä¸”ä¸™å‹è‚ç‚é€šè¿‡è¡€æ¶²ä¼ æ’­æ¯”é€šè¿‡æ€§æ¶²ä¼ æ’­æ›´å®¹æ˜“ï¼Œè¿™æ„å‘³ç€ä½ å€Ÿç”¨åˆ«äººçš„å‰ƒé¡»åˆ€æ¯”é€šè¿‡ä¸ä»–ä»¬è¿›è¡Œå£äº¤æ›´å®¹æ˜“æ„ŸæŸ“â€”â€”ä½†å…±ç”¨å‰ƒé¡»åˆ€é€šå¸¸ä¸æ˜¯ä½“æ¶²ç»“åˆè®¨è®ºçš„ä¸€éƒ¨åˆ†ã€‚

Beyond all this, consider also that fluid bonding wonâ€™t protect you from other kinds of viruses that also transmit between people in close contactâ€”everything from the common cold to COVID-19, which you can share by breathing each otherâ€™s air. As we well know at this point, common airborne viruses can inflict lasting harm and even be deadly. Itâ€™s entirely possible to fully respect a fluid bond, but still pick up a virus at the grocery store that can have devastating consequences for your whole polycule.

é™¤æ­¤ä¹‹å¤–ï¼Œè¿˜è¦è€ƒè™‘ä½“æ¶²ç»“åˆæ— æ³•ä¿æŠ¤ä½ å…å—å…¶ä»–ç±»å‹çš„ç—…æ¯’ä¾µå®³ï¼Œè¿™äº›ç—…æ¯’ä¹Ÿä¼šåœ¨å¯†åˆ‡æ¥è§¦çš„äººä¹‹é—´ä¼ æ’­â€”â€”ä»æ™®é€šæ„Ÿå†’åˆ° COVID-19ï¼Œä½ ä»¬å¯ä»¥é€šè¿‡å‘¼å¸å½¼æ­¤çš„ç©ºæ°”æ¥åˆ†äº«ã€‚ä¼—æ‰€å‘¨çŸ¥ï¼Œå¸¸è§çš„ç©ºæ°”ä¼ æ’­ç—…æ¯’ä¼šé€ æˆæŒä¹…ä¼¤å®³ç”šè‡³è‡´å‘½ã€‚å®Œå…¨æœ‰å¯èƒ½åœ¨å……åˆ†å°Šé‡ä½“æ¶²ç»“åˆçš„åŒæ—¶ï¼Œåœ¨æ‚è´§åº—æ„ŸæŸ“ç—…æ¯’ï¼Œä»è€Œå¯¹æ•´ä¸ªå¤šè¾¹å…³ç³»ç½‘ç»œé€ æˆæ¯ç­æ€§åæœã€‚

None of this is meant to discourage you from coming up with agreements about safer sex that make sense between you and your partners. Rather, we want to encourage you to get really comfortable researching the risks involved in your practices, asking your doctor about them, getting the right tests for you, and coming to agreements with your partners armed with a full slate of information (more about all this shortly). That includes not only discussions about safer sex, but also about safer practices with regard to airborne viruses and anything else that could affect your health as a pair or as a group.

è¿™å¹¶ä¸æ˜¯è¦é˜»æ­¢ä½ ä¸ä¼´ä¾£è¾¾æˆæœ‰æ„ä¹‰çš„å®‰å…¨æ€§è¡Œä¸ºåè®®ã€‚ç›¸åï¼Œæˆ‘ä»¬æƒ³é¼“åŠ±ä½ çœŸæ­£ä¹ æƒ¯äºç ”ç©¶ä½ åšæ³•ä¸­æ¶‰åŠçš„é£é™©ï¼Œå‘ä½ çš„åŒ»ç”Ÿå’¨è¯¢ï¼Œè¿›è¡Œé€‚åˆä½ çš„æ£€æµ‹ï¼Œå¹¶åœ¨æŒæ¡å…¨é¢ä¿¡æ¯çš„æƒ…å†µä¸‹ä¸ä½ çš„ä¼´ä¾£è¾¾æˆåè®®ï¼ˆç¨åä¼šæœ‰æ›´å¤šç›¸å…³å†…å®¹ï¼‰ã€‚è¿™ä¸ä»…åŒ…æ‹¬å…³äºå®‰å…¨æ€§è¡Œä¸ºçš„è®¨è®ºï¼Œè¿˜åŒ…æ‹¬å…³äºç©ºæ°”ä¼ æ’­ç—…æ¯’ä»¥åŠä»»ä½•å¯èƒ½å½±å“ä½ ä»¬ä½œä¸ºä¸€å¯¹æˆ–ä¸€ä¸ªç¾¤ä½“å¥åº·çš„å…¶ä»–æ›´å®‰å…¨åšæ³•çš„è®¨è®ºã€‚

Beyond that, we also think itâ€™s important to acknowledge that no matter how thorough and careful you are, thereâ€™s still a chance youâ€™ll be surprised by an unexpected diagnosis. Viruses donâ€™t judge, they just replicate. It feels really personal when youâ€™re sick, but itâ€™s totally not personal to themâ€”not laden with value judgments, and not targeted because of anyone being â€œbadâ€ or â€œgood.â€ The more you and your partners can take a cue from them and simply try to deal with the reality that we all coexist with viruses, the better youâ€™ll be able to protect yourselves and each other without judgment, and take care of each other with kindness when your best efforts fail.

é™¤æ­¤ä¹‹å¤–ï¼Œæˆ‘ä»¬è¿˜è®¤ä¸ºé‡è¦çš„æ˜¯è¦æ‰¿è®¤ï¼Œæ— è®ºä½ å¤šä¹ˆå½»åº•å’Œå°å¿ƒï¼Œä»ç„¶æœ‰å¯èƒ½å› æ„å¤–çš„è¯Šæ–­è€Œæ„Ÿåˆ°æƒŠè®¶ã€‚ç—…æ¯’ä¸è¯„åˆ¤ï¼Œå®ƒä»¬åªæ˜¯å¤åˆ¶ã€‚å½“ä½ ç”Ÿç—…æ—¶ï¼Œæ„Ÿè§‰çœŸçš„å¾ˆé’ˆå¯¹ä¸ªäººï¼Œä½†å¯¹å®ƒä»¬æ¥è¯´å®Œå…¨ä¸æ˜¯é’ˆå¯¹ä¸ªäººçš„â€”â€”ä¸å¸¦æœ‰ä»·å€¼åˆ¤æ–­ï¼Œä¹Ÿä¸æ˜¯å› ä¸ºä»»ä½•äººæ˜¯â€œåâ€æˆ–â€œå¥½â€è€Œæˆä¸ºç›®æ ‡ã€‚ä½ å’Œä½ çš„ä¼´ä¾£è¶Šèƒ½ä»å®ƒä»¬é‚£é‡Œå¾—åˆ°å¯ç¤ºï¼Œå¹¶ç®€å•åœ°å°è¯•åº”å¯¹æˆ‘ä»¬éƒ½ä¸ç—…æ¯’å…±å­˜è¿™ä¸€ç°å®ï¼Œä½ ä»¬å°±è¶Šèƒ½åœ¨ä¸è¯„åˆ¤çš„æƒ…å†µä¸‹ä¿æŠ¤è‡ªå·±å’Œå½¼æ­¤ï¼Œå¹¶åœ¨ä½ ä»¬å°½æœ€å¤§åŠªåŠ›ä»å¤±è´¥æ—¶å–„å¾…å½¼æ­¤ã€‚

## Wonderful and worrying developments in the STI world
## STI ä¸–ç•Œä¸­ä»¤äººæƒŠå–œå’Œæ‹…å¿§çš„å‘å±•

The last ten to fifteen years have seen some radical new developments in the world of STI prevention and treatment. Here are a few major examples.

åœ¨è¿‡å»ååˆ°åäº”å¹´é‡Œï¼ŒSTI é¢„é˜²å’Œæ²»ç–—é¢†åŸŸå‡ºç°äº†ä¸€äº›æ¿€è¿›çš„æ–°å‘å±•ã€‚ä»¥ä¸‹æ˜¯å‡ ä¸ªä¸»è¦ä¾‹å­ã€‚

* The HPV vaccine, which prevents multiple strains of genital warts, was first released in 2006. Originally it was recommended for girls and young women up to age 26, but today itâ€™s also recommended for boys and young men in that same range, and is available and considered worthwhile for everyone up to age 45. Since its release, the vaccine has reduced the instance of the HPV types that cause most HPV-related cancers and genital warts by over 80 percent among teen girls and young women. These same vaccines prevent over 70 percent of head and neck cancers.[^142]
* A new class of hepatitis C medicines known as direct-acting antivirals was introduced in 2011. Today, these medications can cure more than 90 percent of people in a span of eight to twelve weeks, without the debilitating side effects of the older types of treatments. From its discovery in 1989, hepatitis C now holds the distinction of being the fastest viral disease ever to be identified and cured. It is also the only chronic viral illness that can be completely cured.[^143]
* In 2016, the U = U campaign was launched by an international organization known as Prevention Access Campaign, or PAC. U = U stands for â€œundetectable = untransmittable,â€ and it refers to the fact that when someone who is HIV positive is taking appropriate treatment, their viral load can become so low that itâ€™s undetectable on a test, and at that point, itâ€™s also not possible for them to transmit HIV.[^144]
* Pre-exposure prophylaxis (PrEP), an HIV preventative medication that can be taken orally or by injection, has become widely available. Post-exposure prophylaxis (PEP) has also become widely available. This is a month-long, anti-HIV preventative emergency medication regime that must begin within 72 hours (three days) after possible HIV exposure. Both are effective at preventing HIV infection.[^145]

* HPV ç–«è‹—äº 2006 å¹´é¦–æ¬¡å‘å¸ƒï¼Œå¯é¢„é˜²å¤šç§ç”Ÿæ®–å™¨ç–£èŒæ ªã€‚æœ€åˆå»ºè®® 26 å²ä»¥ä¸‹çš„å¥³å­©å’Œå¹´è½»å¥³æ€§æ¥ç§ï¼Œä½†ä»Šå¤©ä¹Ÿå»ºè®®åŒå¹´é¾„æ®µçš„ç”·å­©å’Œå¹´è½»ç”·æ€§æ¥ç§ï¼Œå¹¶ä¸”å¯¹äº 45 å²ä»¥ä¸‹çš„æ‰€æœ‰äººæ¥è¯´éƒ½æ˜¯å¯ç”¨çš„ä¸”å€¼å¾—æ¥ç§çš„ã€‚è‡ªå‘å¸ƒä»¥æ¥ï¼Œè¯¥ç–«è‹—ä½¿å°‘å¥³å’Œå¹´è½»å¥³æ€§ä¸­å¯¼è‡´å¤§å¤šæ•° HPV ç›¸å…³ç™Œç—‡å’Œç”Ÿæ®–å™¨ç–£çš„ HPV ç±»å‹å‘ç—…ç‡é™ä½äº† 80% ä»¥ä¸Šã€‚è¿™äº›ç–«è‹—è¿˜å¯ä»¥é¢„é˜² 70% ä»¥ä¸Šçš„å¤´é¢ˆç™Œã€‚[^142]
* ä¸€ç±»åä¸ºç›´æ¥æŠ—ç—…æ¯’è¯ç‰©çš„æ–°å‹ä¸™å‹è‚ç‚è¯ç‰©äº 2011 å¹´æ¨å‡ºã€‚å¦‚ä»Šï¼Œè¿™äº›è¯ç‰©å¯ä»¥åœ¨ 8 åˆ° 12 å‘¨å†…æ²»æ„ˆ 90% ä»¥ä¸Šçš„äººï¼Œè€Œä¸”æ²¡æœ‰æ—§ç–—æ³•çš„è¡°å¼±å‰¯ä½œç”¨ã€‚ä» 1989 å¹´è¢«å‘ç°ä»¥æ¥ï¼Œä¸™å‹è‚ç‚ç°åœ¨æ˜¯æœ‰å²ä»¥æ¥è¢«è¯†åˆ«å’Œæ²»æ„ˆé€Ÿåº¦æœ€å¿«çš„ç—…æ¯’æ€§ç–¾ç—…ã€‚å®ƒä¹Ÿæ˜¯å”¯ä¸€å¯ä»¥å®Œå…¨æ²»æ„ˆçš„æ…¢æ€§ç—…æ¯’æ€§ç–¾ç—…ã€‚[^143]
* 2016 å¹´ï¼Œå›½é™…ç»„ç»‡é¢„é˜²è·å–è¿åŠ¨ (Prevention Access Campaign, PAC) å‘èµ·äº† U = U è¿åŠ¨ã€‚U = U ä»£è¡¨â€œæµ‹ä¸åˆ° = ä¼ ä¸äº†â€(undetectable = untransmittable)ï¼ŒæŒ‡çš„æ˜¯å½“ HIV é˜³æ€§è€…æ¥å—é€‚å½“æ²»ç–—æ—¶ï¼Œå…¶ç—…æ¯’è½½é‡å¯ä»¥é™ä½åˆ°æ£€æµ‹ä¸åˆ°çš„æ°´å¹³ï¼Œæ­¤æ—¶ï¼Œä»–ä»¬ä¹Ÿä¸å¯èƒ½ä¼ æ’­ HIVã€‚[^144]
* æš´éœ²å‰é¢„é˜² (PrEP) æ˜¯ä¸€ç§å¯ä»¥å£æœæˆ–æ³¨å°„çš„ HIV é¢„é˜²è¯ç‰©ï¼Œå·²å¹¿æ³›æ™®åŠã€‚æš´éœ²åé¢„é˜² (PEP) ä¹Ÿå·²å¹¿æ³›æ™®åŠã€‚è¿™æ˜¯ä¸€ç§ä¸ºæœŸä¸€ä¸ªæœˆçš„æŠ— HIV é¢„é˜²æ€§ç´§æ€¥è¯ç‰©æ²»ç–—æ–¹æ¡ˆï¼Œå¿…é¡»åœ¨å¯èƒ½æ¥è§¦ HIV å 72 å°æ—¶ï¼ˆä¸‰å¤©ï¼‰å†…å¼€å§‹ã€‚ä¸¤è€…éƒ½èƒ½æœ‰æ•ˆé¢„é˜² HIV æ„ŸæŸ“ã€‚[^145]

These developments are astonishing, and have saved countless lives. The news isnâ€™t all good, though. Just for starters, access to these preventative medications and vaccines is, of course, not universal. Inequities in health care access remain entrenched in many areas of the world, including the United States and Canada. For example, some vaccines are only available if you pay out of pocket, and costs can be prohibitive for some people. Here are some other negative developments:

è¿™äº›å‘å±•ä»¤äººæƒŠè®¶ï¼Œå¹¶æŒ½æ•‘äº†æ— æ•°ç”Ÿå‘½ã€‚ä¸è¿‡ï¼Œå¹¶éå…¨æ˜¯å¥½æ¶ˆæ¯ã€‚é¦–å…ˆï¼Œè·å¾—è¿™äº›é¢„é˜²æ€§è¯ç‰©å’Œç–«è‹—çš„æœºä¼šå½“ç„¶ä¸æ˜¯æ™®éçš„ã€‚åŒ»ç–—ä¿å¥è·å–æ–¹é¢çš„ä¸å¹³ç­‰åœ¨ä¸–ç•Œè®¸å¤šåœ°åŒºä»ç„¶æ ¹æ·±è’‚å›ºï¼ŒåŒ…æ‹¬ç¾å›½å’ŒåŠ æ‹¿å¤§ã€‚ä¾‹å¦‚ï¼Œæœ‰äº›ç–«è‹—åªæœ‰è‡ªè´¹æ‰èƒ½æ¥ç§ï¼Œè´¹ç”¨å¯¹æŸäº›äººæ¥è¯´å¯èƒ½ä»¤äººæœ›è€Œå´æ­¥ã€‚ä»¥ä¸‹æ˜¯ä¸€äº›å…¶ä»–è´Ÿé¢å‘å±•ï¼š

* In 2022, the World Health Organization (WHO) reported that the United States and Canada, among others, have seen an increase in at least three STIs: syphilis, gonorrhea and chlamydia.[^146]
* The WHO further noted increasing outbreaks of â€œnon-classical STIs,â€ meaning illnesses that are spread by a range of means including sexual contact, such as Shigella sonnei, hepatitis A, Neisseria meningitidis, Zika and Ebola. The WHO also reported a re-emergence of what they call â€œneglected STIs,â€ such as lymphogranuloma venereum (LGV) and Mycoplasma genitalium.
* Also in 2022, there was a sudden new spread of mpox outbreaks in major North American cities, chiefly but not exclusively among men who had sexual contact with one another.
* The WHO is also concerned about increasing antimicrobial resistance in Neisseria gonorrhoea and Mycoplasma genitalium, meaning that treatment options may become less effective and more limited.
* And again, letâ€™s not forget COVID-19, which is among the many illnesses transmitted in ways well beyond sexual contact but that certainly include sexual contact. Acute infection is still dangerous, particularly to people who havenâ€™t kept up with vaccinations. Today, nearly 12 percent of the Canadian population is coping with long COVID symptoms, meaning symptoms that endure three or more months after infection,[^147] and many thousands of people are dealing with severe, life-altering disability. COVID-19 isnâ€™t considered an STI, but it certainly affects a lot of peopleâ€™s sex lives!

* 2022 å¹´ï¼Œä¸–ç•Œå«ç”Ÿç»„ç»‡ (WHO) æŠ¥å‘Šç§°ï¼Œç¾å›½å’ŒåŠ æ‹¿å¤§ç­‰å›½è‡³å°‘ä¸‰ç§ STI æœ‰æ‰€å¢åŠ ï¼šæ¢…æ¯’ã€æ·‹ç—…å’Œè¡£åŸä½“æ„ŸæŸ“ã€‚[^146]
* ä¸–å«ç»„ç»‡è¿›ä¸€æ­¥æŒ‡å‡ºï¼Œâ€œéå…¸å‹ STIâ€çˆ†å‘å¢åŠ ï¼ŒæŒ‡é€šè¿‡åŒ…æ‹¬æ€§æ¥è§¦åœ¨å†…çš„å¤šç§é€”å¾„ä¼ æ’­çš„ç–¾ç—…ï¼Œå¦‚å®‹å†…å¿—è´ºèŒã€ç”²å‹è‚ç‚ã€è„‘è†œç‚å¥ˆç‘ŸèŒã€å¯¨å¡ç—…æ¯’å’ŒåŸƒåšæ‹‰ç—…æ¯’ã€‚ä¸–å«ç»„ç»‡è¿˜æŠ¥å‘Šäº†ä»–ä»¬æ‰€è°“çš„â€œè¢«å¿½è§†çš„ STIâ€çš„é‡æ–°å‡ºç°ï¼Œå¦‚æ€§ç—…æ€§æ·‹å·´è‚‰èŠ½è‚¿ (LGV) å’Œç”Ÿæ®–æ”¯åŸä½“ã€‚
* åŒæ ·åœ¨ 2022 å¹´ï¼ŒåŒ—ç¾ä¸»è¦åŸå¸‚çªç„¶çˆ†å‘äº†æ–°çš„çŒ´ç—˜ç–«æƒ…ï¼Œä¸»è¦ä½†ä¸é™äºåœ¨ç”·ç”·æ€§è¡Œä¸ºè€…ä¸­ä¼ æ’­ã€‚
* ä¸–å«ç»„ç»‡è¿˜æ‹…å¿ƒæ·‹çƒèŒå’Œç”Ÿæ®–æ”¯åŸä½“çš„æŠ—èŒç´ è€è¯æ€§å¢åŠ ï¼Œè¿™æ„å‘³ç€æ²»ç–—é€‰æ‹©å¯èƒ½ä¼šå˜å¾—ä¸é‚£ä¹ˆæœ‰æ•ˆå’Œæ›´åŠ æœ‰é™ã€‚
* å†ä¸€æ¬¡ï¼Œåˆ«å¿˜äº† COVID-19ï¼Œå®ƒæ˜¯é€šè¿‡è¿œè¿œè¶…å‡ºæ€§æ¥è§¦çš„æ–¹å¼ä¼ æ’­çš„è®¸å¤šç–¾ç—…ä¹‹ä¸€ï¼Œä½†è‚¯å®šåŒ…æ‹¬æ€§æ¥è§¦ã€‚æ€¥æ€§æ„ŸæŸ“ä»ç„¶å¾ˆå±é™©ï¼Œç‰¹åˆ«æ˜¯å¯¹é‚£äº›æ²¡æœ‰åšæŒæ¥ç§ç–«è‹—çš„äººæ¥è¯´ã€‚å¦‚ä»Šï¼Œè¿‘ 12% çš„åŠ æ‹¿å¤§äººå£æ­£åœ¨åº”å¯¹é•¿æ–°å† ç—‡çŠ¶ï¼Œå³æ„ŸæŸ“åæŒç»­ä¸‰ä¸ªæœˆæˆ–æ›´é•¿æ—¶é—´çš„ç—‡çŠ¶ï¼Œ[^147] æˆåƒä¸Šä¸‡çš„äººæ­£åœ¨åº”å¯¹ä¸¥é‡çš„ã€æ”¹å˜ç”Ÿæ´»çš„æ®‹ç–¾ã€‚COVID-19 ä¸è¢«è§†ä¸º STIï¼Œä½†å®ƒè‚¯å®šå½±å“äº†è®¸å¤šäººçš„æ€§ç”Ÿæ´»ï¼

Considering how quickly STI information can changeâ€”whether weâ€™re talking about new forms of prevention and treatment or new or resurgent STIs to be concerned aboutâ€”we donâ€™t feel that it makes sense to give you a detailed rundown of STI-related information in this book. (Most of the developments above have happened in the ten years since the first edition of this book was published. Who knows what will happen in the next ten!) Instead, we urge you to take a proactive approach to your health in three ways: keeping up to date on the latest scientific research, working with your health care provider to make sure you get appropriate vaccinations and tests based on your practices and the risks that come with them, and practising safer sex with your partners based on ongoing conversations with them about your practices and their associated risks.

è€ƒè™‘åˆ° STI ä¿¡æ¯å˜åŒ–ä¹‹å¿«â€”â€”æ— è®ºæˆ‘ä»¬è°ˆè®ºçš„æ˜¯é¢„é˜²å’Œæ²»ç–—çš„æ–°å½¢å¼ï¼Œè¿˜æ˜¯éœ€è¦å…³æ³¨çš„æ–°å‡ºç°æˆ–æ­»ç°å¤ç‡ƒçš„ STIâ€”â€”æˆ‘ä»¬è§‰å¾—åœ¨æœ¬ä¹¦ä¸­ç»™ä½ ä¸€ä»½è¯¦ç»†çš„ STI ç›¸å…³ä¿¡æ¯æ¸…å•æ˜¯æ²¡æœ‰æ„ä¹‰çš„ã€‚ï¼ˆä»¥ä¸Šå¤§éƒ¨åˆ†å‘å±•éƒ½å‘ç”Ÿåœ¨æœ¬ä¹¦ç¬¬ä¸€ç‰ˆå‡ºç‰ˆåçš„åå¹´é‡Œã€‚è°çŸ¥é“æ¥ä¸‹æ¥çš„åå¹´ä¼šå‘ç”Ÿä»€ä¹ˆï¼ï¼‰ç›¸åï¼Œæˆ‘ä»¬æ•¦ä¿ƒä½ åœ¨ä¸‰ä¸ªæ–¹é¢é‡‡å–ç§¯æä¸»åŠ¨çš„å¥åº·æªæ–½ï¼šåŠæ—¶äº†è§£æœ€æ–°çš„ç§‘å­¦ç ”ç©¶ï¼›ä¸ä½ çš„åŒ»ç–—ä¿å¥æä¾›è€…åˆä½œï¼Œç¡®ä¿ä½ æ ¹æ®ä½ çš„åšæ³•åŠéšä¹‹è€Œæ¥çš„é£é™©è·å¾—é€‚å½“çš„ç–«è‹—æ¥ç§å’Œæ£€æµ‹ï¼›ä»¥åŠæ ¹æ®ä¸ä½ çš„ä¼´ä¾£å°±ä½ çš„åšæ³•åŠå…¶ç›¸å…³é£é™©è¿›è¡Œçš„æŒç»­å¯¹è¯ï¼Œä¸ä»–ä»¬è¿›è¡Œæ›´å®‰å…¨çš„æ€§è¡Œä¸ºã€‚

## STAYING UP TO DATE
## ä¿æŒæ›´æ–° (STAYING UP TO DATE)

When we advise you to do your own research, we donâ€™t mean going down conspiracy-theory rabbit holes based on social media memes. Rather, weâ€™re talking about making a habit of checking a few trusted websites regularly. In Canada, a good source of STI information is CATIE (catie.ca), essentially a clearinghouse for the latest research that aims to disseminate information to health care providers and the general public. You can also check searchable government databases of disease prevention and treatment information, such as the Public Health Agency of Canada (canada.ca/en/public-health/services/diseases.html), the Centers for Disease Control in the United States (cdc.gov/health-topics.html), and others. Planned Parenthood (plannedparenthood.org) and Scarleteen (scarleteen.com/) are also great sources of sexual health and wellness information more generally. Your local sexual health clinic may have an informative website, too.

å½“æˆ‘ä»¬å»ºè®®ä½ è‡ªå·±åšç ”ç©¶æ—¶ï¼Œæˆ‘ä»¬å¹¶ä¸æ˜¯æŒ‡åŸºäºç¤¾äº¤åª’ä½“è¿·å› é™·å…¥é˜´è°‹è®ºçš„å…”å­æ´ã€‚ç›¸åï¼Œæˆ‘ä»¬è¯´çš„æ˜¯å…»æˆå®šæœŸæ£€æŸ¥å‡ ä¸ªå€¼å¾—ä¿¡èµ–ç½‘ç«™çš„ä¹ æƒ¯ã€‚åœ¨åŠ æ‹¿å¤§ï¼ŒSTI ä¿¡æ¯çš„ä¸€ä¸ªå¥½æ¥æºæ˜¯ CATIE (catie.ca)ï¼Œæœ¬è´¨ä¸Šæ˜¯ä¸€ä¸ªæœ€æ–°ç ”ç©¶çš„äº¤æµä¸­å¿ƒï¼Œæ—¨åœ¨å‘åŒ»ç–—ä¿å¥æä¾›è€…å’Œå…¬ä¼—ä¼ æ’­ä¿¡æ¯ã€‚ä½ è¿˜å¯ä»¥æŸ¥çœ‹å¯æœç´¢çš„æ”¿åºœç–¾ç—…é¢„é˜²å’Œæ²»ç–—ä¿¡æ¯æ•°æ®åº“ï¼Œä¾‹å¦‚åŠ æ‹¿å¤§å…¬å…±å«ç”Ÿç½² (canada.ca/en/public-health/services/diseases.html)ã€ç¾å›½ç–¾ç—…æ§åˆ¶ä¸­å¿ƒ (cdc.gov/health-topics.html) ç­‰ã€‚è®¡åˆ’ç”Ÿè‚²åä¼š (plannedparenthood.org) å’Œ Scarleteen (scarleteen.com/) ä¹Ÿæ˜¯æ›´å¹¿æ³›çš„æ€§å¥åº·å’Œä¿å¥ä¿¡æ¯çš„ç»ä½³æ¥æºã€‚ä½ å½“åœ°çš„æ€§å¥åº·è¯Šæ‰€å¯èƒ½ä¹Ÿæœ‰ä¸€ä¸ªä¿¡æ¯ä¸°å¯Œçš„ç½‘ç«™ã€‚

You donâ€™t need to become a researcher or start reading medical journals (unless you want to!). But trust that a lot of progressive organizations are on a mission to help people make good choices about their sexual health and are doing incredible work to get information out there in a way thatâ€™s accessible and useful for the general public. All you have to do is seek it out.

## WORKING WITH YOUR HEALTH CARE PROVIDER
## ä¸ä½ çš„åŒ»ç–—ä¿å¥æä¾›è€…åˆä½œ (WORKING WITH YOUR HEALTH CARE PROVIDER)

Accessing good health care is becoming increasingly difficult in Canada, and has long been a challenge in the United States. Whether itâ€™s under-funded and short-staffed public health services or overpriced private ones, sometimes the barriers may seem insurmountable. Itâ€™s also been clearly established that marginalized people are often treated very poorly in health care settingsâ€”including Black people, Indigenous people, people of colour, people with disabilities, people with addictions and mental illnesses, queer people, poor people, fat people, women, kinky people, sex workers, and anyone with invisible disabilities or rare or episodic symptoms. The specific flavours of mistreatment vary from group to group, but in all cases, members of these groups encounter barriers that more privileged people do not. Itâ€™s also an especially scary and difficult time for trans people, who are often misgendered, denied care or even criminalized for seeking it in some jurisdictions these days. If you experience any of this when you need health care services, all we can say is thatâ€™s rotten, and lots of people are fighting hard to make it better. In the meantime, your self-education and prevention effortsâ€”and the support of your partnersâ€”are all the more crucial, and you may want to make an extra effort to seek out health care services that are tailored to your specific needs and advertise themselves as friendly to nonmonogamous people as well as to people belonging to your specific demographics.

If you have a family doctor or other regular health care provider, great! But even then, itâ€™s no guarantee that theyâ€™re as open-minded as you might need them to be when it comes to your sexual practices, the number of partners you have and your needs for STI testing. This can play out for nonmonogamous people in many ways. Some health clinics, particularly in small towns, have been known to shame people who seek regular STI testing. Many nonmonogamous people do regular screening, yet there is a perception even among some health care professionals that testing is unnecessary for people in stable relationships. Organizations that focus on HIV and sexually transmitted and blood-borne illness education tend to have mandates centred on harm reduction, and have been fighting hard to dismantle stigma and shame for decades now, as well as to stop the criminalization of HIV non-disclosure. But it is an ongoing struggle despite major advances in the prevention and treatment of many STIs. You may need to get pushy to get your needs met.

We believe itâ€™s important to be open with your health care professional about being nonmonogamous, but at the same time, we recognize that some people in the medical community are prejudiced and judgmental about nontraditional relationships, so proceed with caution. You want to strike the balance between being open enough to get good advice and services, and holding whatever boundaries you need in order to feel emotionally safe in your doctorâ€™s office.

Wherever possible, if you encounter stigma or shaming from health care professionals, speak up. Tell them directly that their behaviour is inappropriate. ## TALKING WITH YOUR PARTNERS AND PRACTISING SAFER SEX
## ä¸ä½ çš„ä¼´ä¾£äº¤è°ˆå¹¶è¿›è¡Œæ›´å®‰å…¨çš„æ€§è¡Œä¸º (TALKING WITH YOUR PARTNERS AND PRACTISING SAFER SEX)

In nonmonogamous circles, itâ€™s common for people to be screened for STIs regularly, usually annually and whenever they are considering starting a new sexual relationship, and to talk about test results with a potential new partner before any activity that might involve fluid exchange.

åœ¨éå•å¶åˆ¶åœˆå­é‡Œï¼Œäººä»¬é€šå¸¸ä¼šå®šæœŸè¿›è¡Œ STI ç­›æŸ¥ï¼Œé€šå¸¸æ˜¯æ¯å¹´ä¸€æ¬¡ï¼Œä»¥åŠæ¯å½“ä»–ä»¬è€ƒè™‘å¼€å§‹ä¸€æ®µæ–°çš„æ€§å…³ç³»æ—¶ï¼Œå¹¶åœ¨è¿›è¡Œä»»ä½•å¯èƒ½æ¶‰åŠä½“æ¶²äº¤æ¢çš„æ´»åŠ¨ä¹‹å‰ä¸æ½œåœ¨çš„æ–°ä¼´ä¾£è®¨è®ºæ£€æµ‹ç»“æœã€‚

This approach makes a lot of sense in the context of dating and establishing new relationships. You may also be a good candidate for PrEP. Your local HIV organization or sexual health clinic may provide good advice on safer sex in the context of hookups.

è¿™ç§æ–¹æ³•åœ¨çº¦ä¼šå’Œå»ºç«‹æ–°å…³ç³»çš„èƒŒæ™¯ä¸‹å¾ˆæœ‰æ„ä¹‰ã€‚ä½†å¦‚æœä½ å‚ä¸å…¶ä»–ç±»å‹çš„æ€§æ¥è§¦ï¼Œå¦‚éšæ„å‹¾æ­æˆ–æµ´å®¤ï¼Œä½ å¯èƒ½éœ€è¦æ›´å¤šåœ°ä¾é ä½¿ç”¨å±éšœæˆ–æŒ‘å‰”ä½ å°†å‚ä¸çš„è¡Œä¸ºï¼Œè€Œä¸æ˜¯è¿›è¡Œå¹¿æ³›çš„æ€§å²è®¨è®ºï¼Œå› ä¸ºåè€…ä¸å¤ªå®¹æ˜“èå…¥å¯ç”¨ç©ºé—´å’Œæ—¶é—´ï¼Œä¹Ÿä¸å¤ªç¬¦åˆä»»ä½•ä¸¤ä¸ªäººä¹‹é—´çš„æŠ•å…¥ç¨‹åº¦ã€‚ä½ ä¹Ÿå¯èƒ½æ˜¯ PrEP çš„åˆé€‚äººé€‰ã€‚ä½ å½“åœ°çš„ HIV ç»„ç»‡æˆ–æ€§å¥åº·è¯Šæ‰€å¯èƒ½ä¼šæä¾›å…³äºå‹¾æ­èƒŒæ™¯ä¸‹å®‰å…¨æ€§è¡Œä¸ºçš„è‰¯å¥½å»ºè®®ã€‚

## Pregnancy, the other risk
## æ€€å­•ï¼Œå¦ä¸€ä¸ªé£é™© (Pregnancy, the other risk)

Conversations about safer sex usually revolve around mitigating the risk of STIs, and itâ€™s surprising how many nonmonogamous people donâ€™t talk about pregnancy. It is a fact of nature that anytime sex involves putting sperm in potential contact with an egg, pregnancy sometimes resultsâ€”even, occasionally, when using contraception. This is about cells, so itâ€™s true regardless of the participantsâ€™ gender identities and sexual orientations. In fact, sometimes queer people who are accustomed to same-sex sexual partners can forget that certain types of sex can lead to pregnancyâ€”so if youâ€™re queer and exploring your attractions to people with different plumbing than you, you may need to consider factors youâ€™re not used to when it comes to risks.

å…³äºå®‰å…¨æ€§è¡Œä¸ºçš„å¯¹è¯é€šå¸¸å›´ç»•å‡è½» STI é£é™©å±•å¼€ï¼Œä»¤äººæƒŠè®¶çš„æ˜¯æœ‰å¤šå°‘éå•å¶åˆ¶è€…ä¸è°ˆè®ºæ€€å­•ã€‚è¿™æ˜¯ä¸€ä¸ªè‡ªç„¶äº‹å®ï¼šä»»ä½•æ—¶å€™æ€§è¡Œä¸ºæ¶‰åŠå°†ç²¾å­ä¸åµå­æ½œåœ¨æ¥è§¦ï¼Œæœ‰æ—¶å°±ä¼šå¯¼è‡´æ€€å­•â€”â€”å³ä½¿åœ¨ä½¿ç”¨é¿å­•æªæ–½æ—¶ä¹Ÿæ˜¯å¦‚æ­¤ï¼Œå¶å°”ã€‚è¿™æ˜¯å…³äºç»†èƒçš„ï¼Œæ‰€ä»¥æ— è®ºå‚ä¸è€…çš„æ€§åˆ«è®¤åŒå’Œæ€§å–å‘å¦‚ä½•ï¼Œè¿™éƒ½æ˜¯çœŸçš„ã€‚äº‹å®ä¸Šï¼Œæœ‰æ—¶ä¹ æƒ¯äº†åŒæ€§æ€§ä¼´ä¾£çš„é…·å„¿å¯èƒ½ä¼šå¿˜è®°æŸäº›ç±»å‹çš„æ€§è¡Œä¸ºä¼šå¯¼è‡´æ€€å­•â€”â€”æ‰€ä»¥å¦‚æœä½ æ˜¯é…·å„¿å¹¶ä¸”æ­£åœ¨æ¢ç´¢ä½ å¯¹ä¸ä½ ç”Ÿç†æ„é€ ä¸åŒçš„äººçš„å¸å¼•åŠ›ï¼Œåœ¨æ¶‰åŠé£é™©æ—¶ï¼Œä½ å¯èƒ½éœ€è¦è€ƒè™‘ä½ ä¸ä¹ æƒ¯çš„å› ç´ ã€‚

In all cases, it pays to talk with your partners about pregnancy risks and contingencies. What happens if someone accidentally becomes pregnant?

åœ¨æ‰€æœ‰æƒ…å†µä¸‹ï¼Œä¸ä½ çš„ä¼´ä¾£è°ˆè®ºæ€€å­•é£é™©å’Œæ„å¤–æƒ…å†µéƒ½æ˜¯å€¼å¾—çš„ã€‚å¦‚æœæœ‰äººæ„å¤–æ€€å­•ä¼šå‘ç”Ÿä»€ä¹ˆï¼Ÿ

How do you all feel about abortion? Letâ€™s be super clear that the ultimate decision about whether or not to have an abortion belongs solely with the person who is, or could become, pregnant. However, this is an emotionally charged topic that many people have strong feelings about, and major disagreements here can be relationship-breakers. Itâ€™s best to have the conversations ahead of time so that you arenâ€™t dealing with both a surprise pregnancy and a surprise conflict or breakup at the same time. Itâ€™s also possible that partners may agree on the abortion question, but not on how to proceed once the decision has been made. Some people consider an abortion to be a routine health procedure and have no particularly intense feelings about it, whereas for others itâ€™s a very emotional experience and they need a lot of support. Especially if you live in a place where youâ€™re likely to encounter loud protestors, distressing imagery or laws that require health care providers to discourage you from aborting or provide you with inaccurate medical information, it can be a traumatic experience. Having a loving partner or partners at your side can make a huge difference. Last but not least, abortion is not always easy to access. In some states, itâ€™s illegal or as good as illegal, and in some places in both the United States and Canada, abortion providers are few and far between. So while in some places itâ€™s as simple as booking an appointment, in other places getting an abortion may involve secrecy, expensive travel, time off work and out-of-pocket costs for the procedure itself, which may not be in the budget for everyone. If you could impregnate someone and you live in a place where abortion is hard to access, you may want to consider getting a vasectomyâ€”a procedure that is usually reversible and nearly painless. If you could get pregnant, you may want to consider taking extra contraceptive precautions, such as getting an IUD or taking birth control pills, and having mifepristone at hand (you can order it online) in case of a condom break. Stacking your contraceptive methods can add extra layers of protection in case one should fail.

ä½ ä»¬å¯¹å •èƒæœ‰ä»€ä¹ˆæ„Ÿè§‰ï¼Ÿæˆ‘ä»¬è¦éå¸¸æ¸…æ¥šï¼Œå…³äºæ˜¯å¦å •èƒçš„æœ€ç»ˆå†³å®šæƒå®Œå…¨å±äºæ€€å­•æˆ–å¯èƒ½æ€€å­•çš„äººã€‚ç„¶è€Œï¼Œè¿™æ˜¯ä¸€ä¸ªå……æ»¡æƒ…ç»ªçš„è¯é¢˜ï¼Œè®¸å¤šäººå¯¹æ­¤æœ‰å¼ºçƒˆçš„æ„Ÿå—ï¼Œè¿™é‡Œçš„é‡å¤§åˆ†æ­§å¯èƒ½ä¼šå¯¼è‡´å…³ç³»ç ´è£‚ã€‚æœ€å¥½æå‰è¿›è¡Œå¯¹è¯ï¼Œè¿™æ ·ä½ å°±ä¸ä¼šåŒæ—¶å¤„ç†æ„å¤–æ€€å­•å’Œæ„å¤–å†²çªæˆ–åˆ†æ‰‹ã€‚ä¼´ä¾£ä»¬ä¹Ÿæœ‰å¯èƒ½åœ¨å •èƒé—®é¢˜ä¸Šè¾¾æˆä¸€è‡´ï¼Œä½†åœ¨å†³å®šåšå‡ºåå¦‚ä½•è¿›è¡Œçš„é—®é¢˜ä¸Šä¸ä¸€è‡´ã€‚æœ‰äº›äººè®¤ä¸ºå •èƒæ˜¯å¸¸è§„åŒ»ç–—ç¨‹åºï¼Œå¯¹æ­¤æ²¡æœ‰ç‰¹åˆ«å¼ºçƒˆçš„æ„Ÿå—ï¼Œè€Œå¯¹å¦ä¸€äº›äººæ¥è¯´ï¼Œè¿™æ˜¯ä¸€ç§éå¸¸æƒ…ç»ªåŒ–çš„ä½“éªŒï¼Œä»–ä»¬éœ€è¦å¾ˆå¤šæ”¯æŒã€‚ç‰¹åˆ«æ˜¯å¦‚æœä½ ç”Ÿæ´»åœ¨ä¸€ä¸ªå¯èƒ½ä¼šé‡åˆ°å¤§å£°æŠ—è®®è€…ã€ä»¤äººç—›è‹¦çš„å›¾åƒæˆ–æ³•å¾‹è¦æ±‚åŒ»ç–—ä¿å¥æä¾›è€…åŠé˜»ä½ å •èƒæˆ–å‘ä½ æä¾›ä¸å‡†ç¡®åŒ»ç–—ä¿¡æ¯çš„åœ°æ–¹ï¼Œè¿™å¯èƒ½æ˜¯ä¸€ç§åˆ›ä¼¤æ€§ä½“éªŒã€‚èº«è¾¹æœ‰ä¸€ä¸ªæˆ–å¤šä¸ªå……æ»¡çˆ±çš„ä¼´ä¾£å¯èƒ½ä¼šäº§ç”Ÿå·¨å¤§çš„ä¸åŒã€‚æœ€åä½†å¹¶éæœ€ä¸é‡è¦çš„ä¸€ç‚¹æ˜¯ï¼Œå •èƒå¹¶ä¸æ€»æ˜¯å®¹æ˜“è·å¾—çš„ã€‚åœ¨æŸäº›å·ï¼Œå®ƒæ˜¯éæ³•çš„æˆ–å®é™…ä¸Šæ˜¯éæ³•çš„ï¼Œåœ¨ç¾å›½å’ŒåŠ æ‹¿å¤§çš„æŸäº›åœ°æ–¹ï¼Œå •èƒæä¾›è€…å¯¥å¯¥æ— å‡ ã€‚å› æ­¤ï¼Œè™½ç„¶åœ¨æŸäº›åœ°æ–¹åªéœ€é¢„çº¦å³å¯ï¼Œä½†åœ¨å…¶ä»–åœ°æ–¹ï¼Œå •èƒå¯èƒ½æ¶‰åŠä¿å¯†ã€æ˜‚è´µçš„æ—…è¡Œã€è¯·å‡ä»¥åŠæ‰‹æœ¯æœ¬èº«çš„è‡ªè´¹è´¹ç”¨ï¼Œè¿™å¯èƒ½å¹¶ä¸åœ¨æ¯ä¸ªäººçš„é¢„ç®—ä¹‹å†…ã€‚å¦‚æœä½ èƒ½è®©æŸäººæ€€å­•ï¼Œå¹¶ä¸”ä½ ä½åœ¨ä¸€ä¸ªå •èƒéš¾ä»¥è·å¾—çš„åœ°æ–¹ï¼Œä½ å¯èƒ½æƒ³è€ƒè™‘è¿›è¡Œè¾“ç²¾ç®¡ç»“æ‰æœ¯â€”â€”è¿™æ˜¯ä¸€ç§é€šå¸¸å¯é€†ä¸”å‡ ä¹æ— ç—›çš„æ‰‹æœ¯ã€‚å¦‚æœä½ å¯èƒ½æ€€å­•ï¼Œä½ å¯èƒ½æƒ³è€ƒè™‘é‡‡å–é¢å¤–çš„é¿å­•é¢„é˜²æªæ–½ï¼Œä¾‹å¦‚ä½¿ç”¨å®«å†…èŠ‚è‚²å™¨ (IUD) æˆ–æœç”¨é¿å­•è¯ï¼Œå¹¶æ‰‹å¤´å¤‡æœ‰ç±³éå¸é…®ï¼ˆå¯ä»¥åœ¨çº¿è®¢è´­ï¼‰ä»¥é˜²é¿å­•å¥—ç ´è£‚ã€‚å åŠ ä½ çš„é¿å­•æ–¹æ³•å¯ä»¥å¢åŠ é¢å¤–çš„ä¿æŠ¤å±‚ï¼Œä»¥é˜²ä¸‡ä¸€å…¶ä¸­ä¸€ç§å¤±è´¥ã€‚

Alternatively, would you see an accidental pregnancy as a happy occasion? If so, are all the affected partners in agreement on how they would proceed, what their respective roles would be, and so on? If there is more than one possible impregnator, does it matter who it was? For example, if a person gets pregnant as part of a casual encounter, would they want to raise the child with their nesting partners? As another example, in a triad made up of a cis woman and two cis men, everyone involved may feel itâ€™s important to know who the biological father is, or they may deliberately not want to know, because theyâ€™d like to raise the child as a three-parent unit no matter whose cells were involved in conception. Kinship is a choice, after all. Whatever the specifics of your situation, having a baby is no small undertaking, and itâ€™s worth being deliberate about it if you possibly can.

æˆ–è€…ï¼Œä½ ä¼šæŠŠæ„å¤–æ€€å­•çœ‹ä½œæ˜¯ä¸€ä»¶å¿«ä¹çš„äº‹å—ï¼Ÿå¦‚æœæ˜¯è¿™æ ·ï¼Œæ‰€æœ‰å—å½±å“çš„ä¼´ä¾£æ˜¯å¦å°±åœ¨å¦‚ä½•è¿›è¡Œã€å„è‡ªçš„è§’è‰²ç­‰æ–¹é¢è¾¾æˆä¸€è‡´ï¼Ÿå¦‚æœæœ‰ä¸æ­¢ä¸€ä¸ªå¯èƒ½çš„è‡´å­•è€…ï¼Œæ˜¯è°é‡è¦å—ï¼Ÿä¾‹å¦‚ï¼Œå¦‚æœä¸€ä¸ªäººåœ¨ä¸€æ¬¡éšæ„é‚‚é€…ä¸­æ€€å­•ï¼Œä»–ä»¬ä¼šæƒ³å’Œä»–ä»¬çš„åŒå±…ä¼´ä¾£ä¸€èµ·æŠšå…»å­©å­å—ï¼Ÿå†ä¸¾ä¸€ä¸ªä¾‹å­ï¼Œåœ¨ä¸€ä¸ªç”±ä¸€åé¡ºæ€§åˆ«å¥³æ€§å’Œä¸¤åé¡ºæ€§åˆ«ç”·æ€§ç»„æˆçš„ä¸‰äººç»„ä¸­ï¼Œæ¯ä¸ªå‚ä¸è€…å¯èƒ½éƒ½è§‰å¾—çŸ¥é“è°æ˜¯ç”Ÿçˆ¶å¾ˆé‡è¦ï¼Œæˆ–è€…ä»–ä»¬å¯èƒ½æ•…æ„ä¸æƒ³çŸ¥é“ï¼Œå› ä¸ºæ— è®ºå—å­•æ¶‰åŠè°çš„ç»†èƒï¼Œä»–ä»¬éƒ½æƒ³ä½œä¸ºä¸€ä¸ªä¸‰äº²å®¶åº­æŠšå…»å­©å­ã€‚äº²å±å…³ç³»æ¯•ç«Ÿæ˜¯ä¸€ç§é€‰æ‹©ã€‚æ— è®ºä½ çš„å…·ä½“æƒ…å†µå¦‚ä½•ï¼Œç”Ÿå­©å­éƒ½ä¸æ˜¯ä¸€ä»¶å°äº‹ï¼Œå¦‚æœå¯èƒ½çš„è¯ï¼Œå€¼å¾—æ·±æ€ç†Ÿè™‘ã€‚

No matter what discussions you have, youâ€™re probably going to feel some pretty strong emotions if pregnancy occurs. Pregnancy is a big deal and likely to be disruptive for everyone. Give yourselves time to process your feelings and talk about it together. Donâ€™t postpone the discussion until itâ€™s too late. For more about these issues, how to have these conversations, and how to prepare to raise kids in a nonmonogamous family, see Jess Mahlerâ€™s book *Polyamory and Pregnancy*, listed in the resources.

**QUESTIONS TO ASK YOURSELF**
**é—®è‡ªå·±çš„é—®é¢˜**

Whatever your relationship to sex might be, nonmonogamy is likely to present you with new opportunities and new challenges. We wish you learning, healing and pleasure along the wayâ€”however you define all these things.

æ— è®ºä½ ä¸æ€§çš„å…³ç³»å¦‚ä½•ï¼Œéå•å¶åˆ¶éƒ½å¯èƒ½ä¼šç»™ä½ å¸¦æ¥æ–°çš„æœºä¼šå’Œæ–°çš„æŒ‘æˆ˜ã€‚æˆ‘ä»¬ç¥æ„¿ä½ ä¸€è·¯å­¦ä¹ ã€ç–—æ„ˆå’Œå¿«ä¹â€”â€”æ— è®ºä½ å¦‚ä½•å®šä¹‰æ‰€æœ‰è¿™äº›äº‹æƒ…ã€‚

Here are some questions that might help you figure out where to start or continue your journey!

ä»¥ä¸‹æ˜¯ä¸€äº›å¯èƒ½å¸®åŠ©ä½ å¼„æ¸…æ¥šä»å“ªé‡Œå¼€å§‹æˆ–ç»§ç»­ä½ çš„æ—…ç¨‹çš„é—®é¢˜ï¼

* How would I describe or define my sexualityâ€”my orientation, desires and experiences thus far? What are my likes and dislikes?
* Is there an area related to sex where I could use some care or healing work? What is it, and what strategies could I pursue to feel better? What kinds of resources or help do I want to seek out?
* What new things would I like to explore sexually or learn about sex? What would I hope to get out of those explorations?
* What skills do I want to gain or improve in the realm of communication, negotiation and consent? How can I pursue this learning?
* What are a few sexual boundaries that are important to me?
* What approach do I take to risk in the area of sex, STIs and pregnancy? What do I want to know about a prospective partner before we have sex? What safer sex measures do I want to take? How would I approach a surprise pregnancy? What conversations do I want to have with my partners about these questions?
* What are my strategies for staying up to date on sexual health information and taking care of my sexual health? Do I need to revisit or amend them?
* How do I want to approach sex in the context of nonmonogamy? How does my nonmonogamous situation affect my answers to the other questions in this list, or vice versa?
